category: 'cellular-biology'
dataSource: 'berkeley'

published: true

linksTo: ['']
linkedFrom: ['']
---

<a id="ultrastructure">Ultrastructure</a> - The detailed structure of a specimen, such as a cell, tissue, or organ, that can be observed only by electron microscopy. Also called fine structure. In eggshell, ultrastructure refers to the three-dimensional arrangement of mineral crystals and organic matter. It is described in terms of calcite or aragonite mineralogy and the transition between different zones of organization within the shell. Distinct zones of organization are called ultrastructure zones.

<a id="unicellular">Unicellular</a> - A type of organism which is wholly contained inside a [single cell](#cell). [Bacteria](#bacteria) and [yeast](#yeast) are prime examples of unicellular life.

<a id="uniramious">Uniramious</a> - Among arthropods, uniramous refers to appendages that have only one branch. Insects, centipedes and millipedes, and their relatives are uniramous arthropods; land-living chelicerates such as scorpions, spiders,and mites are also uniramous but probably descended from ancestors with biramous appendages. Contrast with biramous.

---
fileName: 'biology_general-biology_u_untranslated-region-utr.mdx'

category: 'general-biology'
dataSource: 'original-dereck'

---

<a id="untranslated_region_utr">Untranslated Region (UTR)</a> - Sequences in the [5'](#5_prime_end_5) and [3'](#3_prime_end_3) ends of an [mRNA](#mrna) that are [transcribed](#transcription), but not [translated](#translation). UTRs often harbor regulatory elements, for example [protein](#protein) binding sites (including the [ribosome](#ribosome) binding site), [miRNA](#miRna) binding sites, [secondary structures](#conformation) *exempli gratia* [riboswitches](#riboSwitch).


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-

---

<a id="upstream">Upstream</a> - A region of [DNA](#dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid) is upstream of a sequence if it is on the [5'](#5_prime_end_5) side of the sequence of interest, it is always relative to some reference, *exempli gratia* the [promoter](#promoter) is upstream of the coding strand of a [gene](#gene).

The UAS [promoter](#promoter) contains multiple UAS sites and a minimal [CMV](#cytomegalovirus_cmv) core [promoter](#promoter).

<a id="upwelling">Upwelling</a> - The raising of benthic nutrients to the surface waters. This occurs in regions where the flow of water brings currents of differing temperatures together, and increases productivity of the ecosystem.

Stongly activates [gene expression](#geneExpression) in [eukaryotic](#eukaryota) [cells](#cell) when fused to a [DNA](#dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid) binding [protein](#protein), commonly Gal4.

<a id="vacuole">Vacuole</a> - Membrane-bound fluid-filled space within a cell. In most plant cells, there is a single large vacuole filling most of the cell's volume. Some bacterial cells contain gas vacuoles.

---
fileName: 'biology_general-biology_v_variance.mdx'
letter: 'v'
word: 'variance'
dictionary: 'biology'
category: 'general-biology'
dataSource: 'original-dereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['']
linkedFrom: ['']
---

<a id="variance">Variance</a> - A statistical measure describing the spread of a random variable around its mean. A tight distribution has small variance and thus most of the values are close to the mean. The variance is related to the standard deviation by $$ Var(x) = \sigma^2 $$ Mathematically, $$ Var(X) = E\[(X - E\[X\])^2\] $$; the variance is the expectation of the square of the difference of the random variable from its mean. The variance can also be calculated by taking the difference of the expectation of the square of the variable and the expectation of the variable, squared:

 $$ Var(X) = E\[X^2\] - (E\[X\])^2 $$


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'biology_zoology_v_vascular.mdx'

word: 'vascular'

dataSource: 'berkeley'

linkedFrom: ['']
---

<a id="vascular">Vascular</a> - Refers to a network of tubes which distribute nutrients and remove wates from the tissues of the body. Large multicellular animals must rely on a vascular system to keep their cells nourished and alive.

<a id="vector">Vector</a> - A vector is a [DNA](#dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid) molecule that can be used as a carrier of an artificial sequence of interest to be [cloned](#clone) into another cell.

---
fileName: 'biology_general-biology_v_vesicle.mdx'
letter: 'v'
word: 'vesicle'
dictionary: 'biology'
category: 'general-biology'
dataSource: 'original-dereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['lipidBilayer' ,'dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid' ,'rna' ,'protein' ,'nucleic_acid' ,'endocytosis']
linkedFrom: ['']
---

<a id="vesicle">Vesicle</a> - Vesicles are [lipid bilayer](#lipidBilayer) structures that contain liquid and materials of interest. [DNA](#dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid), [RNA](#rna) and [proteins](#protein) can all be delivered to cultured cells using vesicles created in a mixture of the desired payload. As the vesicles form, they encapsulate the [nucleic acids](#nucleic_acid) and [proteins](#protein) in the mixture. The vesicles can merge with the cell outer layer or be [endocytosed](#endocytosis), both leading to the delivery of the payload (though through different biological mechanisms). Reagents are commercially available for this purpose, optimised for different payload types, amounts, and sizes.


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-

fileName: 'biology_phylogenetics_v_vicariance.mdx'

---

<a id="vicariance">Vicariance</a> - Speciation which occurs as a result of the separation and subsequent isolation of portions of an orginal population.


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'biology_general-biology_v_virus.mdx'
letter: 'v'
word: 'virus'
dictionary: 'biology'
category: 'general-biology'
dataSource: 'original-dereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['protein' ,'gene' ,'host' ,'dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid' ,'rna' ,'positive_rna_rna' ,'enveloped_virus']
linkedFrom: ['antibody','cytomegalovirus_cmv','dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid','enveloped_virus','oncolytic','rna_interference_rnai','strain']
---

<a id="virus">Virus</a> - A virus can be simply explained as a [protein](#protein) and [genetic](#gene) material complex. These are pseudoorganisms and there is much debate as to whether they should or not be classified as living. Viruses have no metabolism and are incapable or reproduction without parasitic use of a [host cell](#host)'s biological machinery. Some viruses may contain [DNA](#dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid) or [RNA](#rna) in the case of [RNA](#rna) it may be either [positive RNA](#positive_rna_rna) or negative.

Viruses are complex "organisms" with a variety of forms, shapes, and sizes. Some present simple parasitic cycles while others integrate completely into the [host](#host)'s [DNA](#dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid) as HIV does. See [enveloped virus](#enveloped_virus).


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'biology_general-biology_w_watson-strand-5-3.mdx'
letter: 'w'
word: 'watson-strand-5-3'
dictionary: 'biology'

linksTo: ['sense_sequence' ,'crick_strand' ,'mrna']

---

<a id="watson_strand_5_3">Watson Strand (5' â 3')</a> - The [sense strand](#sense_sequence) complementary to the [Crick strand (3' â 5')](#crick_strand). This strand matches the [mRNA](#mrna) sequence after T-S are replaced with U-S.


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'biology_general-biology_w_western-blot-immunoblot.mdx'
letter: 'w'
word: 'western-blot-immunoblot'
dictionary: 'biology'
category: 'general-biology'
dataSource: 'original-dereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['protein' ,'electrophoresis' ,'antibody']
linkedFrom: ['electrophoresis']
---

<a id="western_blot_immunoblot">Western Blot (immunoblot)</a> - A lab technique used for detecting the expression of specific [proteins](#protein) in a tissue sample or extract.

 The first step is generally to migrate the proteins by a classic [electrophoretic gel](#electrophoresis), then to "blot" the gel with a membrane (normally nitrocellulose or polyvinyldene fluoride) which holds [antibodies](#antibody) specific to the [protein](#protein) of interest. This will only reveal the bands which contained said [protein](#protein).

<a id="whorl">Whorl</a> - An arrangement of appendages, such as branches or leaves, such that all are equally spaced around the stem at the same point, much like the spokes of a wheel or the ribs of an umbrella

fileName: 'biology_general-biology_x_x-gal.mdx'
letter: 'x'
word: 'x-gal'
dictionary: 'biology'
category: 'general-biology'
dataSource: 'original-dereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['galactose' ,'derivative' ,'cleavage' ,'Î²_galactosidase_lactase_Î²_gal_beta_gal' ,'lacz' ,'indole' ,'dimer']
linkedFrom: ['galactosidase_gal','reporter','Î²_galactosidase_lactase_Î²_gal_beta_gal','Î²_galactosidase_lactase_Î²_gal_beta_gal']
---

<a id="x_gal">X-Gal</a> - A colourless [galactose](#galactose) [derivative](#derivative). When [cleaved](#cleavage) by [Î²-galactosidase](#Î²_galactosidase_lactase_Î²_gal_beta_gal) (encoded by the [LacZ gene](#lacz)), gives rise to two products: [galactose](#galactose) and an [indole](#indole). [Indole](#indole) spontaneously forms a [dimer](#dimer) which has a blue colour. This allows to test for the presence of [Î²-galactosidase](#Î²_galactosidase_lactase_Î²_gal_beta_gal) activity and by extension the activity or presence of the [LacZ gene](#lacz).


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'biology_botany_x_xylem.mdx'
letter: 'x'
word: 'xylem'

<a id="yeast">Yeast</a> - A class of mostly [unicellular](#unicellular) [eukaryotic](#eukaryota) organisms. These easy-to-culture organisms are often used in biology and [synthetic biology](#synthetic_biology) as test organisms or chassis. Commonly used yeast species include [*Saccharomyces cerevisiae*](#sachharomyces_cerevisiae) (brewer's yeast or baker's yeast; unlike other [eukaryotes](#eukaryota) it is missing the [RNAi](#rna_interference_rnai) machinery). Some notable species are *Kluyveromyces lactis* which can convert lactose to lactic acid, and *Schizosaccharomyces pombe* fission yeast; this yeast has [RNAi](#rna_interference_rnai) machinery.

<a id="zinc_finger_zf">Zinc Finger (ZF)</a> - A [DNA](#dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid) binding protein motif, often seen in [transcription factors](#transcription_factor_tf). In the folded motif, a zinc ion is coordinated by [amino acid](#amino_acid) helices and sheets. Many motifs bind sequence-specifically (3-4 [base pairs](#base_pair_bp)) and can be combined together to bind longer sequences (9-12 basepairs). While a "code" exist (mapping of motifs to sequences), technologies like [TALEs](#taleS) or [CRISPR/Cas9](#crispr_cas9) are often preferred for engineering [DNA](#dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid) binding [proteins](#protein), as the code is more clear and specific in these; increasing the rate of success.

<a id="Î²_cells">Î²-Cells</a> - Î²-cells are a type of [cell](#cell) found in the pancreas, the pancreatic islets more precisely. These [cells](#cell) are responsible for the synthesis and secretion of [insulin](#insulin) and [amylin](#amylin).

 They make up around 50 - 70% of all the cells in the islets. People with type-I diabetes have their Î²-cells iradicated and thus produce no insulin.

<a id="Î²_galactosidase_lactase_Î²_gal_beta_gal">Î²-Galactosidase (lactase, Î²-gal, beta-gal)</a> - Is a glycoside hydrolase enzyme which catalyses the breakdown of Î²-galactosides into monosaccharides. It is commonly used in molecular biology as a [reporter gene](#reporter) to monitor gene expression. Microorganisms, or other, producing this enzyme can break down [x-gal (5-bromo-4-chloro-3-indolyl-Î²-d-galactopyranoside)](#x_gal), an artificial chromogenic substrate. Cleavage of this substrate produces a blue colour. It can be mixed in [petri dishes](#petri_dish); known as [blue/white screening](#blueWhiteScreening).

 This enzyme can be split into two complimentary peptides LacZÎ± and LacZÎ©; neither active by itself but together can break down [x-gal](#x_gal).

---

<a id="2d_electrophoresis">2D Electrophoresis</a> - A 2D electrophoresis, is a variant of the [electrophoresis](#electrophoresis) lab method. Usually the first [electrophoresis](#electrophoresis) is done to migrate proteins based on their mobility which is dependent on the charge/mass ratio. Then a second migration is done in the other dimension, or perpendicular to the first; now migrating the proteins based on their [potential hydrogen isoelectric point (pHi)](#potential_hydrogen_isoelectric_point_phi). By doing this we can reveal different proteins that might have been hidden all in one band.

<a id="3_prime_end_3">3 Prime End (3')</a> - The 3' or 3 prime end of a [DNA](#dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid) or [RNA](#rna) molecule is the end that holds the hydroxyl group (\-OH). The other end of a nucleic strand is the [5' or 5 prime end ](#5_prime_end_5), which has the phosphate group (\-PO4). For an illustration see the figure for [DNA](#dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid).

<a id="5_prime_end_5">5 Prime End (5')</a> - The 5' or 5 prime end of a [DNA](#dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid) or [RNA](#rna) molecule is the end that holds the terminal phosphate (\-PO4). This allows for the formation of the [phosphodiester bond](#phosphodiesterBond) between two [nucleotides](#nucleotide). The other end of a nucleic strand is the [3' or 3 prime end ](#3_prime_end_3), which has the hydroxyl group (\-OH). For an illustration see the figure for [DNA](#dna_desoxyribonucleic_acid).

---
fileName: 'chemistry_general-chemistry_a_acid.mdx'
letter: 'a'
word: 'acid'
dictionary: 'chemistry'
category: 'general-chemistry'
dataSource: 'dereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['Hydrogen']
linkedFrom: ['ph_potential_hydrogen']
---

<a id="acid">Acid</a> - An acid as per the BrÃ¸nsted definition is any substance capable of giving off a proton, or [Hydrogen](#Hydrogen) particle. As per the Lewis definition an acid is any substance capable of accepting a pair of electrons.

 Notice that these two definitions are complementary, in that if: an acid gives off a proton, it is releasing a charged particle H+ into the environment; yet at the same time it must accept those electrons 2e\- from the proton in order to release it.

 An acid is written as AH and its conjugated base as A\-. The couple is written as: AH/A\-.

 They are linked together by the two half reactions:

 $$ AH \rightleftharpoons A^- + H^+ $$ $$ B^- + H^+ \rightleftharpoons BH $$

 Thus the two half reactions together form the complete reaction:

 $$ AH + B^- \rightleftharpoons A^- + BH $$

 Notice that the acid is giving up its H to the base, and in exchange the A receives the negative charge \- which in fact represents the two electrons given up by the Hydrogen.

 If one were to follow the electrons they go from: H to A, and from the B to the H in order to form the bond.


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-

---

<a id="amphoteric">Amphoteric</a> - The quality of a substance to be able to act as both an acid and a base. This is an innate quality of water and is seen in [autoprotolysis](#autoprotolysis).


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'chemistry_organic-chemistry_a_antiaromatic.mdx'

linksTo: ['']

---

<a id="antiaromatic">Antiaromatic</a> - If a molecule has 4n Ï electrons and has the all other characteristics for aromaticity then it is said to be antiaromatic.
1. The molecule must be cyclic.
2. The molecule must be planar, allows for p-orbitals to be parallel and interact.
3. That the molecule must be formed of a continous ring of conjugated Ï bonds (p-orbital interaction); if a heteronome participates in conjugation then its doublet must be able to be conjugated with the other Ï bonds (can be composed of one or more rings).

 Example: cyclobutadiene; the number of Ï delocalised electrons is 4, its ionic form cyclobutadiene(2\-) is aromatic (6 e\-).


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'chemistry_general-chemistry_a_autoprotolysis.mdx'
letter: 'a'
word: 'autoprotolysis'
dictionary: 'chemistry'
category: 'general-chemistry'
dataSource: 'dereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['']
linkedFrom: ['amphoteric','dissociation_constant_water','dissociation_constant_water']
---

<a id="autoprotolysis">Autoprotolysis</a> - A chemical reaction occuring in water between water molecules themselves H2O in which a proton is transfered between two identical molecules, one playing the role of an acid and the other the base (in the BrÃ¸nsted context).

 The chemical reaction is written:

 $$ 2H_2 O \rightleftharpoons H_3 O^+ + HO^- $$

 Any solvent which contains acidic hydrogen and lone pairs of electrons with which to accept H+ can demonstrate autoprotolysis, for example ammonia:

 $$ 2NH_3 \rightleftharpoons NH^-_2 + NH^+_4 $$


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'chemistry_organic-chemistry_a_aromaticity-aryl.mdx'
letter: 'a'
word: 'aromaticity-aryl'
dictionary: 'chemistry'
category: 'organic-chemistry'
dataSource: 'origDereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['']
linkedFrom: ['']
---

<a id="aromaticity_aryl">Aromaticity (aryl)</a> - This is the property of being cyclic (a closed ring), and planar with a ring of resonance bonds. Aromatic chemical groups are called aryl. Requirements for aromaticity, known as Huckel's rule, are as follows:
1. The molecule must be cyclic; can be composed of one or more rings.
2. The molecule must be planar, allows for p-orbitals to be parallel and interact.
3. That the molecule must be formed of a continous ring of conjugated Ï bonds (p-orbital interaction); if a heteronome participates in conjugation then its doublet must be able to be conjugated with the other Ï bonds.
4. The molecule must have 4n + 2 electrons in a conjugated system (n â â); normally on sp2\-hybridised atoms, and sometimes sp-hybridised.

 To apply the rule, count the number of Ï eletrons in the molecule, then set the equation equal to this number. Solve for n. If n â â, and the other rules are true then the molecule is indeed aromatic.

 Example: benzene, 3 double bonds, 6 Ï electrons. $$ 4n + 2 = 6 $$ $$ 4n = 4 $$ $$ n = 1 $$ $$ n \in \mathbb{N} $$ In order to know which are the Ï electrons to be counted, you must look at those which reside in the p-orbitals; the hybrid atoms have one p-orbital each. Thus if each part of the cyclic compound is hybridised sp2 then this means that the molecule is completely conjugated (each atom has one p-orbital) and the electrons in these p orbitals are in fact Ï electrons.

 Aromatic molcules are very stable and do not react easily with other compounds. Most common aromatic compounds are derivatives of benzene (found in petroleum).

 Aromaticity does not necessarily indicate an odour. This misnomer comes from the association initially given by August Wilhelm Hofmann in 1855 who studied a class of benzene molecules; which did indeed emit odours.

Benzene resonance structures.

 Chemically aromaticity refers to a conjugated system made of alternating single and double bonds in a ring. This arrangement allows for electrons in the molecule's Ï system to be delocalised; increasing the molecules stability. Such molecules are represented by a resonance hybrid of different strucutres.

 Bond lengths in such a strucutre are the intermediate of the two composing representations. X-ray diffraction has shown that all six carbon-carbon bonds in benzene are of the same length: 1.4 â«. C-C double bonds are typically at 1.35 â«, and single bonds at 1.47 â«.

The Ï orbitals allow for the double bond stick out of the ring, this allows for the double bond formation; consequently these orbitals can then share any electrons involved.

 The ring contains Ï bonds which are formed from the overlap of atomic p-orbitals; which can interact with each other freely as they are outside the plane of the molecule. Each electron is shared by all six atoms in the ring; there are not enough electrons to form double bonds between all carbon atoms, but the "extra" electrons strengthen the exisiting bonds and give rise to the resonance structure.


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'chemistry_general-chemistry_b_base.mdx'
letter: 'b'
word: 'base'
dictionary: 'chemistry'
category: 'general-chemistry'
dataSource: 'dereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['Hydrogen']
linkedFrom: ['ph_potential_hydrogen']
---

<a id="base">Base</a> - As per the BrÃ¸nsted definition it is any substance capable of taking a proton, or [Hydrogen](#Hydrogen) particle. As per the Lewis definition a base is any substance capable of releasing a pair of electrons.

 Notice that these two definitions are complementary, in that if a base takes on a proton, it is accepting a charged particle H+ from the environment; yet at the same time it must release its electrons 2e\- and share them with the proton in order to form a bond.

 A base is written as B\- and its conjugated acid as BH. The couple is written as: B\-/BH.

 They are linked together by the two half reactions:

 $$ AH \rightleftharpoons A^- + H^+ $$ $$ B^- + H^+ \rightleftharpoons BH $$

 Thus the two half reactions together form the complete reaction:

 $$ AH + B^- \rightleftharpoons A^- + BH $$

 Notice that the base is accepting an H from the acid, and in turn the A receives the negative charge \- which in fact represents the two electrons given up by the Hydrogen.

 If one were to follow the electrons they go from hydrogen to the A, then from B to the H in order to form the bond.


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-

<a id="benzene_c6h6">Benzene (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>6</sub>)</a> - An aromtaic organic molecule of the formula C6H6, molar mass 78.114 gÂ·mol\-1. It is cyclic and contains only Carbon and Hydrogen thus it is called a hydrocarbon.

Various representations of benzene including the hybrid resonance strucutre.

 Benzene is naturally found in petroleum and is a foundational chemical in petrochemistry: production of plastics, pharmaceuticals, dyes, detergents, pesticides, rubber, and other important molecules.

A chart demonstrating the various uses for benzene and derivative molecules possible.

 It is a liquid, colourless, highly volatile, flammable, and a known human carcinogen.

---
fileName: 'chemistry_general-chemistry_d_dissociation-constant-water.mdx'
letter: 'd'
word: 'dissociation-constant-water'
dictionary: 'chemistry'
category: 'general-chemistry'
dataSource: 'dereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['autoprotolysis']
linkedFrom: ['']
---

<a id="dissociation_constant_water">Dissociation Constant (water)</a> - The rate at which water dissociates from its protons. This can be derived from [autoprotolysis](#autoprotolysis).

 Dissociation constant: $$ K_w = 1.0 \cdot 10^{-14} $$ 

 $$ K_w = \frac{\[H_{3}0^{+}\]\[HO^-\]}{\[ H_{2}0\]^2} $$

 Since this is theoretically measured in a pure solution of water and the concentration of pure substances is not taken into account then the formula simplifies to:

 $$ K_w = \[H_{3}0^{+}\]\[HO^-\] $$

 Moreover, since according to the balanced equation for [autoprotolysis](#autoprotolysis) \[H3O+\] = \[HO\-\], and thus we can simplify the formula even more:

 $$ K_w = X^2 $$

 Thus:

 $$ \[H_{3}0^{+}\] = \[HO^-\] = \sqrt{1.0 \cdot 10^{-14}} = 1.0 \cdot 10^{-7} mol/L $$

 And thus we've demonstrated that water's $$ pH = -log(1.0 \cdot 10^{-7}) = 7 $$.


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-

fileName: 'chemistry_general-chemistry_h_hydronium-ion.mdx'
letter: 'h'
word: 'hydronium-ion'
dictionary: 'chemistry'

<a id="hydronium_ion">Hydronium Ion</a> - An oxygen cation attached to three protons, its composition is written as H3O+. This is different from R3O+ known as a [oxonium ion](#oxonium_ion) and instead of three protons can include any other groups. A [hydronium ion](#Hydronium_Ion) is an oxonium ion, but the inverse is not true; as this term refers specifically to ions which have hydrogens.


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'chemistry_general-chemistry_o_oxonium-ion.mdx'

word: 'oxonium-ion'

---

<a id="oxonium_ion">Oxonium Ion</a> - An oxonium ion is any oxygen cation with three bonds, the simplest form is H3O+ but in reality can be R3O+; R meaning any other groups. An oxonium ion is not a [hydronium ion](#Hydronium_Ion), this term refers specifically to ions which have hydrogens.


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'chemistry_general-chemistry_p_ph-potential-hydrogen.mdx'
letter: 'p'
word: 'ph-potential-hydrogen'
dictionary: 'chemistry'
category: 'general-chemistry'
dataSource: 'dereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['acid' ,'base' ,'hydronium_ion' ,'oxonium_ion']
linkedFrom: ['poh_potential_hydroxide']
---

<a id="ph_potential_hydrogen">pH (Potential Hydrogen)</a> - Loosely speaking a measure of the concentration of free protons in an aqueous solution. A more precise definition is the activity of protons in the solution or a measure of \[H3O+\]. This allows us to determine the [acidity](#acid) or [basicity](#base) of a solution. These measurements are all put in the context of water, and relative to water; thus water's pH is equal to 7; acids are less than 7, bases are greater than 7.

 In a base: $$ \[HO^-\] > H_3 O^+ $$ In an acid: $$ \[HO^-\] < H_3 O^+ $$
- Hydroxide ions = \[OH\-\]
- Hydronium ions = \[H3O+\]

 pH is measured on a logarithmic scale, it is measured in units of moles per liter of hydrogen ions often called [hydronium ions](#hydronium_ion) sometimes less precisely called [oxonium ions](#oxonium_ion).

 Precisely speaking this measurement of hydrogen ions in a solution can be done experimentally. In fact an experimental measure is designated as p\[H\]. Moreover, the difference between pH and p\[H\] is so small that they are sometimes used interchangeably. The way to find this would be by use of the Nernst equation as shown below:

 $$ E = E^{0} + f\frac{2.303RT}{F}\log\left\[ H^{+} \right\] $$

 The strict definition of pH does not have associated units, and is dimensionless. It is described as the log of the inverse of hydrogen activity in a solution:

 $$ pH = -log(a_{H^+}) = log\left(\frac{1}{a_{H^+}}\right) $$

 But most often used in the following form with \[H3O+\] being the concentration of hydronium ions in mol/L:

 $$ pH = -log\[H_3 O^+\] $$ $$ \[H_3 O^+\] = 10^{-pH} $$


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'chemistry_general-chemistry_p_poh-potential-hydroxide.mdx'
letter: 'p'
word: 'poh-potential-hydroxide'
dictionary: 'chemistry'
category: 'general-chemistry'
dataSource: 'dereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['ph_potential_hydrogen']
linkedFrom: ['']
---

<a id="poh_potential_hydroxide">pOH (Potential Hydroxide)</a> - The inverse of pH, this measures the potential hydroxide activity of a solution: HO\-.

 $$ pOH = -log\[OH^-\] $$ $$ \[OH^-\] = 10^{-pOH} $$

 Note that the sum of pOH along with [pH](#ph_potential_hydrogen) is equal to the total of the scale.

 $$ pH + pOH = 14 $$


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-


---
fileName: 'chemistry_general-chemistry_#_Ï-bond-pi-bond.mdx'
letter: '#'
word: 'Ï-bond-pi-bond'
dictionary: 'chemistry'
category: 'general-chemistry'
dataSource: 'dereck'

published: true

linksTo: ['']
linkedFrom: ['']
---

<a id="Ï_bond_pi_bond">Ï bond (Pi bond)</a> - These are covalent chemical bonds where two lobes of an orbital on one atom overlap with two other lobes of another laterally. The Greek letter Ï is used to refer to the p-orbitals. The symmettry of the Ï bond is the same as that of the p-orbital; Ï bonds occur with p-orbitals and or d-orbitals.

 Ï bonds are weaker than Ï bonds, requiring 612 kj of energy to break vs 348 kj respectively, that is 264 kj required to break a Ï bond; as the Ï bond is comprised in the 612 kj initially quoted \*(C-C bonds).

 Single covalent bonds result in Ï bonds, multiple bonds yeild Ï bonds. These also result in unsaturated molecules, as electrons are used for multiple bond formation rather than fixing H. Note that Ï bonds do not allow free rotation around the central axis as Ï bonds do.


-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-:-
